We are working to extend the well-known observer performance evaluation methodology of receiver operating characteristic (ROC) analysis ( Egan, 1975; Metz, 1978 ) to classification tasks with three or more classes. This could conceivably be of benefit, for example, in a medical decision-making task in which a region of a patient image must be characterized as containing a malignant lesion, a benign lesion, or only normal tissue ( Chan, Sahiner, Hadjiiski, Petrick, & Zhou, 2003; Edwards, Lan, Metz, Giger, & Nishikawa, 2004 ); or in a task in which multiple types of abnormality or defect must be distinguished from each other and from benign or normal cases ( He, Metz, Tsui, Links, & Frey, 2006 ).

Unfortunately, a fully general extension of ROC analysis to classification tasks with more than two classes has yet to be developed, for the following reasons. It is known that the performance of an observer in a classification task with  
                         N 
                       classes ( 
                         N 
                         ≥ 
                         2 
                      ) can be completely described by a set of  
                         
                            
                               N 
                            
                            
                               2 
                            
                         
                         − 
                         N 
                       conditional error probabilities ( Van Trees, 1968 ), and that the performance of the ideal observer (that which minimizes Bayes risk ( Van Trees, 1968 )) is completely characterized by an ROC hypersurface in which these conditional error probabilities depend on a set of  
                         
                            
                               N 
                            
                            
                               2 
                            
                         
                         − 
                         N 
                         − 
                         1 
                       decision criteria ( Edwards, Metz, & Kupinski, 2004 ). Although analytic expressions for the ideal observer’s conditional error probabilities given reasonable models for the underlying observational data have been worked out in the two-class case ( Metz & Pan, 1999 ), this has not yet been accomplished in a fully general manner for tasks with three or more classes. Furthermore, we have shown that an obvious generalization of the area under the ROC curve (AUC) does not in fact yield a useful performance metric in tasks with three or more classes ( Edwards, Metz, & Nishikawa, 2005 ). More recently, we showed that complicated constraining relationships exist among the decision boundaries themselves for the ideal observer ( Edwards & Metz, 2005 ). These constraining relationships appear to imply that it is highly unlikely that analytical expressions for the conditional error probabilities in terms of the decision criteria can be developed which are as simple to interpret as those for the two-class task.

Despite the difficulties just described, the potential benefits to be gained from a practical performance evaluation methodology for classification tasks with three classes have motivated a number of research groups to propose such methods. These practical methods reduce the number of degrees of freedom used to describe the observer’s performance, either by implicitly leaving the remaining degrees of freedom out of the analysis, or by explicitly imposing restrictions on the form of the observer’s decision rule or on the set of decision criteria used by the observer. An example of the former would be the work of  Mossman (1999)  and of  Dreiseitl, Ohno-Machado, and Binder (2000) , which proposed reporting performance only in terms of the three “sensitivities” (conditional classification rates for correctly identifying the three classes) in a three-class task, while examples of the latter would include the works of  Scurfield (1996, 1998) , of  Chan et al. (2003)  and  Sahiner, Chan, and Hadjiiski (2006) , of  He et al. (2006)  and  He and Frey (2006) , and of  Schubert, Thorsen, and Oxley (2011) . (Recently, He et al. have pointed out ( He, Gallas, & Frey, 2010 ) that, given complete analytic knowledge of a three-class ideal observer’s “sensitivity” ROC surface, whose coordinates are the three correct classification probabilities, one can recover the probability density functions (pdfs) of the pair of likelihood ratios used by that observer. These pdfs, in turn, can be used to recover a complete description of the observer’s performance, including the misclassification ROC surface. However, the practical consequences of this theoretical result have yet to be fully investigated; and for the present, we are concerned here with a situation in which the underlying data pdfs are already assumed known, obviating the need for such an intermediate step via measurement of the sensitivity surface.)

The first work by Scurfield mentioned above ( Scurfield, 1996 ), and that of  Schubert et al. (2011) , are of particular interest because they consider models which assume not only a particular form for the observer’s decision rule, but also that the underlying observational data, for which decisions are being made, are univariate. Such a univariate model is worth exploring for both theoretical and pragmatic reasons. Since a univariate model will be more tractable, in general, than a multivariate model, the former can serve as an important step in the development of a fully general classification and performance evaluation methodology; although the development of a suitable performance metric and evaluation methodology is beyond the scope of the present work, this, which is our long-term goal. More pragmatically, a variety of medical decision-making tasks can be envisioned in which the underlying data consist of a single variable: the result of a particular blood test; or the size of a lesion in a context where other features of the lesion can be ignored, such as a skin test for allergies or tuberculosis. The psychophysical task considered by Scurfield was the ability of an observer to distinguish monotonic auditory stimuli into “low tone”, “medium tone”, and “high tone” classes based on perceived frequency; the clinical task considered by Schubert et al. was to distinguish chronic allograft nephropathology from normal kidney function and proteinuria based on protein markers present in patient urine.

The decision rule of interest here, considered in both papers just mentioned is defined by a pair of thresholds on the underlying data; observations less than the lower threshold are assigned to one class, those between the two thresholds to a second class, and those above the higher threshold to a third class. This is a straightforward generalization of two-class classification models (such as the conventional ( Metz, Herman, & Shen, 1998 ) and proper ( Metz & Pan, 1999 ) binormal models; recall that the latter is motivated by an ideal observer approach to the underlying data model of the former) in which a single threshold is used to separate univariate data into two classes. While more complicated observer behavior is certainly conceivable, it is perhaps surprising that the three-class ideal observer itself is not limited to such a decision strategy. For normally distributed underlying data, in particular, we show here that, depending on the values of its five decision criteria and the distributional parameters of the underlying data, the three-class ideal observer operates by dividing the underlying data axis into as many as seven regions (i. e., by dividing the underlying data axis with as many as six thresholds). This is by no means intended as a criticism of the works already cited, which do not directly address the ideal observer; the particular observer models developed in those works were explicitly assumed to apply to arbitrary decision strategies, as opposed to specifically ideal observer decision strategies.

A univariate decision variable model in a task with more than two classes was investigated also by  Kijewski, Swensson, and Judy (1989) . However, evaluation was performed only using standard two-class ROC analysis on pairs of classes.

In Section  2 , we review the theory of the three-class ideal observer, and develop explicit expressions for the ideal observer’s likelihood ratio decision variables in the case of normally distributed underlying data. In particular, because the two decision variables are functions of univariate data, it is shown that one of those decision variables can be expressed as a relation of the other (a curve, open or closed, in the decision variable space). Because the form of this relation between the decision variables is determined by the relative means and variances of the three pdfs of the underlying data, conditional on membership in each of the three classes, we consider three distinct situations: in Section  3 , the case of all three variances being equal; in Section  4 , the case in which two of the three variances are equal, and the third is distinct from them; and in Section  5 , the case in which all three variances are distinct. In Section  6 , these results are used to develop closed-form expressions for the ROC operating points of the ideal observer under consideration. In Section  7 , we summarize some general properties of parametric surfaces that will be of use in subsequent sections. In Section  8 , we apply these properties to the ROC surface at hand to obtain constraints on the number of degrees of freedom of that surface. In Section  9 , we discuss the most salient qualitative features of the preceding results; and finally, the conclusions of the present work are given in Section  10 .

If all three variances are equal, then by the arguments preceding Eq.  (6) , we can assume without loss of generality that they are all unit variances. For the purpose of labeling the classes, however, it is more useful in this particular case to select the labels of the classes such that

We can safely assume here that no two means are equal, because otherwise at least two of the normal distributions of the underlying data would have both equal means and equal variances; those resulting distributions would thus be identical, observations drawn from those two distributions would be indistinguishable even in principle, and the problem would be at most a two-class classification problem. (This is equivalent to the case in the conventional ( Metz et al., 1998 ) and proper ( Metz & Pan, 1999 ) binormal models in which the curve parameters are  
                         a 
                         = 
                         0 
                       and  
                         b 
                         = 
                         1 
                      , and the only possible classification method is equivalent to random “guessing”.)

It is clear from this “data space” representation of the ideal observer’s decision rule that it is indeed valid to consider, in a fashion similar to two-class classification, the behavior of the ideal observer decision strategy to be a simple partitioning of the underlying data space via a set of thresholds.

The distinction made here between the decision criteria,  
                         
                            
                               γ 
                            
                            
                               j 
                               i 
                               j

For the example of  Fig. 1 , finding the relevant data space thresholds could be done analytically; in the more complicated cases of subsequent sections, it can in principle always be done numerically. Since we are concerned in this work, however, with developing a broad understanding of the behavior of an ideal observer acting on three-class univariate normal data, we will leave aside the details of such calculations, taking as given that they can indeed be carried out (as was of course done to generate the figures in this paper). We emphasize here that these data space thresholds are dependent on, and thus less fundamental than, the decision criteria  
                         
                            
                               γ 
                            
                            
                               j 
                               i 
                               j 
                            
                         
                       with which the ideal observer makes decisions. Although the choice of values for these criteria in a particular task is an important issue, it is beyond the scope of the present work. Here we seek to characterize the range of behavior available to the three-class ideal observer (much as, in a two-class task, the ROC curve represents the range of behavior available to the observer, and the choice of the appropriate operating point on this curve must be made based on factors “external” to the curve itself, i. e., the appropriate choice of  
                         
                            
                               γ 
                            
                            
                               j 
                               i 
                               j 
                            
                         
                       and thus of the utilities  
                         
                            
                               U 
                            
                            
                               i 
                               | 
                               j 
                            
                         
                      ).

In the present example it is possible to exploit the simple form of the decision boundaries, noting that the boundary

Fig. 2  is similar to those presented by other researchers when considering the three-class classification of univariate data (see Fig. 1 of  Scurfield (1996) , to name one example). Partitioning the data space into three regions with two thresholds is a straightforward and intuitive extension of the behavior of a typical two-class observer, which partitions the data space into two regions with a single threshold. Even in the two-class case, however, intuition can occasionally be misleading; it is known, for example, that the behavior of the two-class ideal observer, even for univariate normal data, will (except in the special case of equal variances of the two classes) partition the data space with a  pair  of thresholds, with one class being decided for observations between the thresholds, and the other decided for observations outside them ( Metz & Pan, 1999 ). One of the basic purposes of this paper is to demonstrate that the behavior of the three-class ideal observer also involves more complex situations than implied by  Fig. 2 . Perhaps counterintuitively, this can be shown even in the case of equal variances; consider the same underlying data as before (

Although less intuitive than the decision boundaries shown in  Fig. 1 , such a choice of decision criteria does in fact represent valid behavior of the three-class ideal observer ( Edwards & Metz, 2005 ). In  Fig. 5 (a), it is evident that the given decision boundaries intersect the likelihood ratio curve in four places (although in general, depending on the location of the intersection point and slopes of the decision boundaries, they could of course intersect in fewer than four places).                             x

For a given set of decision criteria, one can calculate or estimate the nine classification probabilities  
                         P 
                         
                            ( 
                            
                               d 
                            
                            = 
                            
                               
                                  π

                               i 
                               j

Since these are conditional probabilities, we can eliminate three of them; we have shown previously ( Edwards & Metz, 2007; Edwards et al., 2005 ) that the performance of any observer in a three-class classification task can be completely described using the six possible misclassification probabilities  
                         
                            
                               P 
                            
                            
                               i 
                               j

                            i 
                            ≠ 
                            j

                               P 
                            
                            
                               3 
                               j 
                            
                         
                       ( Chan et al., 2003; Sahiner et al., 2006 ); however, our long-term goal is a general performance evaluation method not subject to such simplifications.

As the ideal observer’s decision criteria  
                         
                            
                               γ 
                            
                            
                               j 
                               i 
                               j 
                            
                         
                       are swept through their possible values, the resulting operating points sweep out a surface in the ROC space whose coordinates are the six misclassification probabilities. Moreover, since the set of decision criteria can be multiplied by any constant scale factor without changing the decision boundary line segments of Eqs.  (3)–(5) , there are only five degrees of freedom amongst the  
                         
                            
                               γ 
                            
                            
                               j 
                               i 
                               j 
                            
                         
                      , and thus at most five degrees of freedom along such a “misclassification” ROC surface in the most general case ( Edwards et al., 2004 ).

Although the details of the above expressions depend strongly on the parameters of the underlying data  
                         
                            (

                               γ 
                            
                            
                               j 
                               i 
                               j

                         ⋯

                               6 
                            
                         
                       can be found numerically, as stated in Section  3 . The ROC operating point coordinates corresponding to this choice of decision criteria is then given by equations of the form of Eqs.  (38)–(42) . Of course, as shown in Sections  3–5 , it may be the case that, for a given set of values of the underlying data parameters and a particular set of decision criteria, there may actually be fewer than six such intersection points of the likelihood ratio curve and the decision boundary line segments. In that case, the expressions for the  
                         
                            
                               P 
                            
                            
                               i 
                               j 
                            
                         
                       will also be simpler, but will still follow the basic pattern shown above, i. e., sums of differences of values of  
                         Φ 
                         
                            ( 
                            ⋅ 
                            ) 
                         
                      .

We claimed at the end of Section  3  that the behavior of the ideal observer can be shown to be quantitatively different from that of the “intuitive” observer which partitions the data space into three regions with two thresholds ( Schubert et al., 2011; Scurfield, 1996 ). We are now in a position to verify this explicitly, for the first example considered by Schubert et al.. It will also prove convenient to be able to generalize the concept of the Jacobian to include situations in which the number of coordinates in the parametric spaces of interest are not necessarily the same. That is, we will refer to a matrix of the above form, with elements  
                         ∂ 
                         
                            
                               P

                         ∂

                               j

                               u 
                            
                            
                               j 
                            
                         
                      , the resulting parametric “surface” in the ROC space with coordinates  
                         
                            
                               P 
                            
                            
                               i 
                               j 
                            
                         
                       will be degenerate (having fewer than five degrees of freedom, analogous to a curve in a three-dimensional space).

In Sections  3–5 , and  Appendix A , we show that, for univariate trinormal data, the decision boundary line segments used by the ideal observer can intersect the likelihood ratio curve in at most six places; an example of such a situation is shown in  Fig. 8 (a). More generally, the number of such intersections depends in a complicated way on the parameters of the underlying data (the means and variances of the data pdfs) and on the choice of decision criteria governing the decision boundaries (the  
                         
                            
                               γ 
                            
                            
                               j 
                               i 
                               j 
                            
                         
                       of Eqs.  (3)–(5)  or, equivalently, the boundary slopes and intersection point

                            → 
                         
                       determined by these parameters). We show in this section that the rank of the Jacobian of Eq. More particularly, the behavior of  
                         
                            
                               J 
                            
                            
                               m 
                               → 
                               P 
                            
                         
                       falls into three categories: one when there are six such intersection points; another when there are four or fewer intersection points; and a third when there are five intersection points.

It will prove useful to note first that a given set of decision boundary parameters

Consider the case in which there are six intersection points of the decision boundaries with the likelihood ratio curve, an example of which is given in  Fig. 8 (a). In  Appendix A  we show that in such a case, one of the decision boundary line segments must fail to intersect the likelihood ratio curve at all.                                →

It is, furthermore, not difficult to see that this special case, in fact, adequately represents the general situation when there are exactly five intersection points of the decision boundaries and the likelihood ratio curve. As explained in Section  8.1 , if the intersection point of the three decision boundaries lies outside the likelihood ratio curve, then  
                            
                               
                                  J 
                               
                               
                                  m 
                                  → 
                                  P 
                               
                            
                          will fail to be full rank. On the other hand, if the intersection point of the three decision boundaries lies inside the likelihood ratio curve, then it must be the case that one of the decision boundaries intersects the likelihood ratio curve in three places, and the other two boundaries intersect in one place each. (The five intersection points cannot be distributed with two on two of the boundaries and one on the remainder, because then the intersection point of the three decision boundaries cannot consistently be defined as either inside or outside the likelihood ratio curve.) For such a situation, careful examination of the argument of  Appendix B  will show that  
                            
                               
                                  J 
                               
                               
                                  m 
                                  → 
                                  x

                                  x 
                               
                               
                                  j

                                  m 
                               
                               
                                  j

                            ∂ 
                            / 
                            ∂

                                  i 
                                  j 
                               
                            
                          of the corresponding ROC operating point via equations of the form of Eqs.  (38)–(42) . By the arguments of Section  7 , the resulting ROC surface must have fewer than five tangent vectors, i. e., degrees of freedom, at this operating point. Noting the generality of this result (i. e., its applicability to any set of decision criteria such that the decision boundaries intersect the likelihood ratio curve in five places), and combining it with the results of Sections  8.1 and 8.2 , we are forced to conclude that the ROC surface of a three-class ideal observer for univariate trinormal data must everywhere have strictly fewer than five degrees of freedom.

As implied in Section  1 , the behavior of the ideal observer even for a simple, restricted underlying data model can be quite complicated, particularly when viewed in terms of that underlying data. However, we would like to reiterate the point made in Section  4.1 , that one of the goals of this paper was not to focus on the process of determining the numerical values of the data space thresholds on  
                         x 
                      , but rather to develop a more qualitative but broad understanding of the ideal observer’s behavior under this data model. Again, the analogy made in Section  3  with the two-class proper binormal model ( Metz & Pan, 1999 ) seems apt: although the determination of the pair of thresholds on  
                         x 
                       for a given decision criterion is an important practical issue, it is of less theoretical interest than the observation that the ideal observer (in the simpler representation) acts by comparing a single decision variable, the likelihood ratio, with that single decision criterion.

Thus the determination of the particular data space thresholds (the “b” subfigures of this paper) for a given set of decision criteria, and of the relations among those thresholds, though daunting, should be regarded primarily as a computational issue, of considerably less importance than the behavior of the ideal observer (the “a” subfigures)–that is, the choice of decision criteria  
                         
                            
                               γ 
                            
                            
                               j 
                               i 
                               j 
                            
                         
                      –which leads to those sets of thresholds. In  Appendix A , we prove that the maximum number of such intersection points, and thus thresholds on  
                         x 
                      , is indeed six as seen in Section  5 .

In that vein, we note one qualitative feature of interest regarding the likelihood ratio curve not mentioned in the preceding sections. In Figs.  7 (a) and  8 (a), one can see a “hook” in the likelihood ratio curve as it approaches the

This proved confusing when graphs we produced during our preliminary investigations appeared to have no such hook, despite the variances of the underlying data distributions being different. Further investigation soon revealed that a hook was indeed present, but was simply too small to be easily seen in the initial version of the graphs in question. This situation can be compared with the well-known observation that a “hook” is always present in the ROC curve of an observer under the conventional binormal model as long as the variance of the two underlying data distributions are different; however, when the difference between the variances is small enough, the hook may become difficult to observe ( Metz & Pan, 1999 ). Although this analogy is appropriate in the sense that in both cases, the presence or absence of the hook in question is a result of variances of the underlying data distributions being unequal or equal, it is important to note that the “hook” under discussion here is a characteristic of the behavior of the ideal observer itself, and not of a comparison with non-ideal observer behavior as in the two-class ROC curve’s “hook”.

One of the numerous obstacles to the successful extension of ROC analysis to classification tasks with more than two classes has been the difficulty in developing a suitable performance metric for evaluating observer performance in such tasks. Since our discovery that the obvious generalization of the AUC performance metric, the hypervolume under the ROC surface, fails to be a useful performance metric for tasks with more than two classes ( Edwards et al., 2005 ), we have invested much effort in developing such a suitable performance metric ( Edwards & Metz, 2007, 0000,? ). Although we have argued that there are theoretical grounds for expecting our proposed performance metric to address some of the drawbacks inherent in the hypervolume performance metric, our proposed metric nevertheless relies on the assumption that the ROC surface is non-degenerate, i. e., possesses five degrees of freedom, over some non-empty region of support in the domain of the ROC space.

Recently, however, the conjecture has been raised that the ROC hypersurface of an arbitrary observer might fail to have five degrees of freedom, even if the observer in question is an ideal observer acting on multivariate data ( He & Frey, 2009 ). It is certainly the case that we have been heretofore unable to calculate explicitly ROC operating points for an arbitrary three-class ideal observer (i. e., one making decisions for underlying data with arbitrary pdfs), let alone tangent vectors along such an ROC surface. In this sense, the present work represents a significant step toward addressing the issue of the number of degrees of freedom of ideal observer ROC surfaces in general. Despite the outcome being in this case a negative one–in the sense that we have indeed shown that for univariate trinormal underlying data, the ideal observer’s ROC surface has fewer than five degrees of freedom–we believe the insights we have developed in the course of this work will allow us to make further progress in addressing the more general issue.

It is worth pointing out that an objection in the opposite direction could also be raised: the present study involved only univariate trinormal underlying data; is it not possible that univariate but not normally distributed data could be found such that the ideal observer’s ROC surface has five degrees of freedom? We consider this possibility unlikely. The arguments of Section  8.2 , for example, are clearly independent of the underlying data pdfs involved; if there are fewer than five intersection points for a given choice of decision criteria, then no local manipulation of those decision criteria will be able to yield five linearly independent tangent vectors at that operating point. Similarly, if there are exactly five intersection points, then it should still be the case as discussed in Section  8.3  that three of them lie on one decision boundary, and one on each of the other two; although the details would be different from the trinormal case, the resulting Jacobian should still fail to be full rank for the reasons stated. Finally, it is at least in principle conceivable that a set of underlying data exists such that the resulting likelihood ratio curve is sufficiently convoluted to allow six or more intersection points with the decision boundary lines without the intersection of the decision boundaries lying outside the likelihood ratio curve. However, in a practical situation, the available observational data would have to justify the additional complexity entailed in such a model as compared with the univariate trinormal model.

Although a fully general three-class extension to ROC analysis has yet to be developed, it is to be hoped that insights obtained in the consideration and analysis of constrained or simplified models will prove useful in the development of such fully general methodology. In the present paper, we have investigated the behavior of the three-class ideal observer under a particular constrained underlying data model, a univariate trinormal model. By categorizing the various possible situations given the values of the parameters (the means and variances) of this underlying data model, we were able to “catalog” the possible behaviors of the ideal observer’s decision variables under this model.

This in turn allows one to make statements regarding the behavior of the ideal observer itself; in particular, we showed that given the constraints on the possible shapes of the likelihood ratio curve, the decision boundaries used by the ideal observer can intersect that curve in at most six places. The ideal observer nature of this model was demonstrated by comparison with a non-ideal decision rule investigated by other researchers; as expected, the ideal observer attains a lower cost (greater expected utility) for a given set of decision criteria and data model parameters.

We anticipate that these results will prove of use in the further development of a fully general extension of ROC analysis to classification tasks with three or more classes. In particular, extending the underlying data model from the univariate trinormal one considered here to a bivariate or multivariate trinormal model should provide sufficient flexibility to model the behavior of observers, such as algorithmic or human observers, more relevant to typical medical decision-making tasks, including the medical imaging tasks mentioned in the introduction.

We have also shown that, for the underlying data model considered here, the ROC surface must always be degenerate; that is, one always has fewer than five linearly independent tangent vectors to the ROC surface, and thus the ROC surface has fewer than five degrees of freedom, at any operating point. Although this implies that the development of a suitable performance metric for classification tasks with three or more classes may prove an even greater challenge than we had previously demonstrated, we believe the insights we have gained through the work reported here will prove invaluable in addressing this more general issue.

The authors would like to thank Craig Abbey, whose questions and comments on this subject inspired us to investigate it more thoroughly than we had previously.

This work was supported by grant  R01 EB000863  from the  National Institutes of Health  (Kevin Berbaum, the University of Iowa, principal investigator) through a subcontract from the University of Iowa to the University of Chicago (Charles Metz, subcontract principal investigator).
